Founders experience each day as a life-or-death moment that outsiders never see.** ([source](greycroft_mark_terbeek_outliers))

> *"The second slide is what it really looks like and it's this incredibly jagged squiggly line up and down with like heartbeats and death signs and the reality is like even if it didn't quite look like that it feels like that to the founders."*

**Implication:** Resilience frameworks and mental health support for founders matter more than polished strategy decks — the internal experience of the journey is far more volatile than external metrics suggest.

**The vast majority of potential entrepreneurs never start — they turn back at the first moment of perceived risk.** Of the tiny fraction who do jump in, most fail quickly, and only a rare few make it through. The startup mortality curve is steep, but even the ones who 'sink' gain irreplaceable experience. ([source](greycroft_mark_terbeek_outliers))

> *"995 of the kids are like no way I'm not doing that they just turn around they go back into the locker room there's five knuckleheads that stay out there... two immediately just sink to the bottom and drown two kind of lash their arms around the lane line and just kind of hang there and somehow like one makes it to the shallow end."*

**Implication:** The scarcity of successful startups is not primarily a talent problem but a willingness-to-try problem — the biggest filter is the courage to start, not the ability to execute.

**The long game is the defining competitive posture for building durable companies.** Vision must be big enough and progress visible enough to sustain team belief through inevitable choppiness — the combination of compelling vision plus measurable forward motion is what keeps founders and talent aligned during hard stretches. ([source](greycroft_mark_terbeek_outliers))

> *"Keep the passion and the vision and just kind of keep rolling and try to just stay levelheaded through the choppiness... as long as the vision is really compelling and big and there's progress even if it's choppy those seem to be the requisite things to keep people excited and fired up."*

**Implication:** Leaders should invest as much in communicating and refreshing the company vision as they do in short-term execution — vision is not decoration, it is the fuel that sustains effort through difficulty.

**Founding a company is the most complete self-discovery process available.** Regardless of outcome, founders join an elite club of people who understand themselves — their strengths, weaknesses, and emotional range — at a depth unavailable to those who never took the plunge. ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

**Implication:** The intrinsic value of founding — self-knowledge and resilience — is independent of financial outcome. Investors who have founded companies carry a form of empathy that is structurally inaccessible to those who haven't.

**The actual trajectory of building a company feels like a jagged, emotionally violent line — not a smooth upward curve.** Even highly successful founders experience near-death moments, existential doubt, and daily emotional whiplash. ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

> *"There's this incredibly jagged squiggly line up and down with like heartbeats and death signs and the reality is like even if it didn't quite look like that it feels like that to the founders. As a founder I remember like every day I'd wake up and somebody would send an email and there's some story of a new company that sounds like it's doing something similar and your heart drops."*

**Implication:** Founders should normalize the emotional chaos of building rather than comparing themselves to sanitized success narratives. Investors who understand this can provide more grounded support during difficult periods.

**Playing the long game — maintaining vision, passion, and level-headedness through inevitable choppiness — is the distinguishing characteristic of companies that become durable category leaders.** ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

> *"Keep the passion and the vision and just kind of keep rolling and try to just stay level-headed through the choppiness. It's hard to do but that's the part that I think the best companies are able to keep — as long as the vision is really compelling and big and there's progress, even if it's choppy, those seem to be the requisite things."*

**Implication:** Endurance through volatility — not initial idea quality — separates companies that reach category leadership from those that flame out. Vision clarity is the anchor that makes grit sustainable.

**Feedback during the messy middle must be consumed with a temporal filter.** Negative feedback from users or the market in the early messy middle often reflects the discomfort of encountering something new — not a signal that the thing is wrong. Positive feedback, conversely, can be dangerously misleading if it comes from early adopters whose behavior won't generalize. The discipline of the middle is knowing which feedback to act on, which to file, and which to discount entirely. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Build a feedback taxonomy for your team: what kinds of feedback demand immediate response, what should be tracked as a pattern, and what should be logged but not acted on? Undisciplined feedback responsiveness in the messy middle leads to product churn, strategy drift, and teams that feel like they're always running but never arriving.

**The messy middle is the volatile, nonlinear space between the initial excitement of starting something and the eventual outcome. Most ventures fail not because the underlying idea was flawed but because the people behind them quit during this phase. Endurance — not strategy, talent, or funding — is the primary variable separating those who finish from those who don't. The leader's core job in this phase is to survive it long enough for the idea to become reality.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Stop diagnosing failure as an idea problem. Most startup post-mortems misattribute cause. If you're in the messy middle right now, the most important question isn't 'Is this the right strategy?' — it's 'Do we have the psychological infrastructure to keep going?'

**The messy middle is not a straight line downward or upward — it is jagged, with unpredictable highs and lows.** The goal isn't to eliminate the volatility but to achieve a positive slope within it: each low should be slightly less devastating than the one before, and each high should reach a little further. A leader who can engineer that gradual upward trend across the chaos is actually making progress, even when it doesn't feel like it. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Reframe how you measure progress during hard stretches. If your lows are getting marginally less catastrophic and your recoveries are getting incrementally faster, that is meaningful evidence of progress — even if you're nowhere near the finish line.

**Volatility in a startup is not a sign that something is broken — it is often the very signature of something special.** The wild swings between despair and euphoria that characterize the messy middle are frequently what make a company worth building. Attempts to smooth out the volatility through premature professionalization, consensus processes, or defensive management often kill the creative energy that made the venture compelling in the first place. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Resist the instinct to hire your way to stability prematurely. Bringing in 'professional management' to calm volatility before product-market fit is often a way of trading the thing that makes your company interesting for the thing that makes it safe — and safe early-stage companies rarely become great ones.

**The final mile of completing a project is an entirely different sport from everything that came before it.** The skills, mindsets, and team configurations that got a venture 90% of the way to completion become liabilities in the final stretch. Finishing requires different people, different decisions, and a willingness to ruthlessly kill features and ideas you are most emotionally attached to — at exactly the moment when emotional attachment is highest. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Audit your team and your own tendencies when you're close to finishing something. The people who are best at pioneering and exploring are often the worst at closing and shipping. Deliberately recruit for finishing energy, and give people who are great closers the authority to cut what isn't ready.

**One of the key psychological traps in the messy middle is the 'comparison treadmill' — measuring your internal chaos against the polished external presentation of competitors. Every company looks more put-together from the outside than it actually is. The team that appears to have everything figured out is navigating its own messy middle; you're just not seeing their internal reality.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Stop benchmarking your emotional state against other companies' press releases. Competitive anxiety in the messy middle almost always draws on false data. The most useful comparisons are longitudinal ones — how does your current position compare to your own position six months ago, not to a competitor's curated narrative?

**The messy middle demands that leaders simultaneously hold two contradictory skills.** the ability to optimize — cutting what isn't working, sharpening what is — and the ability to endure — resisting the urge to abandon things prematurely before they've had the chance to prove themselves. These two capacities pull in opposite directions, and confusing them leads to either premature quitting or zombie projects that should have been killed. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Develop an explicit internal framework for distinguishing between a project that needs more time and one that needs to be killed. The signals are different: a time problem shows gradual improvement despite setbacks; a structural problem shows repeated failure of the same mechanism regardless of effort invested.

**One of the underappreciated sources of failure in the messy middle is the accumulation of organizational debt — unresolved conflicts, unclear ownership, and misalignment that compounds silently until it becomes catastrophic. Teams avoid these conversations because confrontation feels costly in the short term. But the deferred cost is almost always higher: by the time organizational debt becomes visible, it typically requires restructuring that would have been trivially easy to address months earlier.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Schedule regular 'organizational debt reviews' the way you schedule technical debt reviews. Make explicit the unresolved conflicts, unclear decision rights, and misaligned incentives in your team. Addressing them costs far less when the organization is healthy than when it's under the stress of a growth sprint or a crisis.

**Perseverance in the messy middle is not the same as stubbornness.** Perseverance means continuing despite difficulty when the underlying thesis remains valid. Stubbornness means continuing despite evidence that the thesis has been invalidated. The distinction matters enormously: some of the most costly entrepreneurial failures involve founders who persevered when they should have pivoted, and some of the most costly pivots involved founders who quit when they should have persevered. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Build a regular thesis-testing ritual into your messy-middle operations. Separate the question 'Are we willing to keep going?' from the question 'Is the underlying thesis still valid?' Answer the second question honestly before using the first as justification. These are different decisions that require different data.

**Recognition and small wins function as psychological fuel in the messy middle.** Leaders who wait to celebrate until the destination is reached deprive teams of the incremental reinforcement that sustains long-haul effort. Deliberately engineering small milestones, calling out progress explicitly, and marking intermediate achievements creates a rhythm of accomplishment that makes the grinding plateau survivable. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Design milestone architecture into long projects deliberately — not just business milestones, but psychological ones. Define what a 'win' looks like at the end of each month and celebrate it loudly. Teams that feel like they're winning small, consistently, will keep going long after teams that are waiting to feel like winners.

**The most dangerous person in the messy middle is not the skeptic but the passenger — someone who has disengaged emotionally from the mission but continues to occupy space and draw resources. Unlike vocal skeptics, who create productive friction, passengers drain energy without contributing it. Belsky has argued that the clearest act of leadership in the middle phase is making it easy for passengers to leave and impossible for the mission to be diluted by their presence.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Create clear re-enrollment moments — points in your operating cycle where team members explicitly recommit to the mission or are helped to find a better fit. Don't confuse retention with engagement. A smaller, fully enrolled team almost always outperforms a larger team with significant passenger population.

**The messy middle is where competitive advantages are actually built, not at launch.** The companies that survive the middle phase develop operational capabilities, team cohesion, and institutional knowledge that cannot be replicated by a well-funded competitor simply by throwing resources at the problem. The suffering is the moat — teams that have endured hard things together have a shared resilience that makes them significantly harder to displace. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) by Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Reframe the messy middle as competitive infrastructure being built in real time. Every crisis your team navigates together, every near-death experience you survive, and every hard decision you make under uncertainty deposits something into your organizational resilience account that compounds over time and is genuinely difficult to copy.

**Energy management matters as much as time management in the messy middle.** Leaders who run at maximum intensity without deliberate recovery become unable to make good decisions precisely when good decisions matter most. Belsky has documented that some of his worst leadership choices came during periods of prolonged exhaustion — not because the analytical capacity failed, but because the emotional regulation required for bold decisions eroded first. ([source](Scott Belsky — How to Conquer the Messy Middle | The Tim Ferriss Show (Podcast)))

**Implication:** Treat your own energy as a strategic asset, not a personal indulgence. Build recovery into the operating rhythm of long projects, not as a reward for finishing but as a prerequisite for finishing well. The team that sleeps, recovers, and maintains perspective will outlast the team that grinds without pause.

**The rules that make a company successful in one phase of the messy middle become precisely the wrong playbook in the next phase. The lean, fast, decisive culture that works at ten people breaks at one hundred. The consensus-driven, process-oriented culture that works at one thousand would have killed the company at ten. Effective leaders recognize these phase transitions and actively discard habits and practices that no longer fit — even when those habits feel like core identity.** ([source](Scott Belsky — How to Conquer the Messy Middle | The Tim Ferriss Show (Podcast)))

**Implication:** Run periodic audits of your operating norms and ask honestly: were these rules written for the company we were, or the company we are? The leaders who fail at scale almost never fail for lack of ability — they fail because they are running a small-company playbook on a large-company problem.

**The beginning of any venture is characterized by a burst of optimism and energy that is almost chemically induced — the excitement of possibility creates a false sense of certainty. This initial high is a terrible predictor of eventual success. The real test of a venture begins only after that energy dissipates and the team confronts the grinding plateau where novelty has worn off and results haven't yet materialized.** ([source](Scott Belsky: Lessons from the Messy Middle of a Startup's Journey))

**Implication:** Be suspicious of early enthusiasm as a signal. The moments when a project feels most alive — right at launch or ideation — are actually the moments with the least information. Build structures, habits, and accountability systems during the highs that will carry you through the inevitable troughs.

**Indecision is one of the most underrated killers of momentum in the messy middle.** Teams rationalize delay as careful deliberation, but prolonged indecision compounds the psychological weight of the middle phase and creates organizational paralysis. A mediocre decision made quickly and adjusted rapidly is almost always better than a perfect decision made too late — because momentum itself is a resource that gets depleted by waiting. ([source](The Danger of Indecision - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Establish explicit decision deadlines for your team and yourself. The cost of a wrong decision that gets corrected in two weeks is almost always lower than the cost of waiting six weeks for certainty. Make the bias toward action a cultural value, not just a stated one.

**Behance itself was born out of a long messy middle — Belsky spent years building the platform with limited traction before it gained meaningful adoption. Rather than treating this period as evidence of failure, he understood it as the price of admission for any creative endeavor that is trying to change behavior. The experience became the source material for the frameworks he would later codify in The Messy Middle.** ([source](Scott Belsky on Starting Behance, Perseverance in Business, & the 'Messy Middle' | #AskGaryVee 290))

**Implication:** Your current grind is your future credibility. The founders and leaders who endure the messy middle and eventually emerge are the ones with earned authority to teach, write, and invest from a place of genuine experience rather than theoretical abstraction.

**The idea trap — the tendency to keep generating new ideas rather than executing on existing ones — is one of the primary failure modes Belsky identified in creative professionals and founders. Generating a new idea produces a neurochemical reward that executing on an old one doesn't. This creates a systematic bias toward starting over rather than finishing, and it means the messy middle gets abandoned not because the idea failed but because a new idea felt more exciting.** ([source](Scott Belsky: How To Avoid The Idea Generation Trap))

**Implication:** Create structural barriers to starting new initiatives before existing ones reach completion or explicit kill decisions. The most productive creative organizations have rituals for finishing, not just rituals for ideation. Reward completion as visibly and loudly as you reward ideation.

**Being willing to be bad at things that don't define you is a prerequisite for being great at the things that do.** In the messy middle, resource constraints force choices about where to invest quality. Leaders who try to be excellent at everything spread attention so thin that they become mediocre at everything. Accepting deliberate under-performance in non-critical areas is a strategic act, not a failure of standards. ([source](Scott Belsky: What Are You Willing to Be Bad At?))

**Implication:** Make an explicit list of the two or three things your venture must be exceptional at and everything else it is allowed to be bad at. This isn't a rationalization for sloppiness — it's a resource allocation discipline that protects your highest-leverage investments from being diluted by the gravitational pull of the non-essential.

**Constraints in the messy middle are not obstacles to endurance — they are often the engine of it.** Teams and founders who operate with abundant resources frequently lose the resourcefulness that makes early-stage companies dynamic. Scarcity forces creative problem-solving, tighter prioritization, and stronger team cohesion around what actually matters. Belsky has argued that building in deliberate constraint, even when capital is available, produces better long-term outcomes. ([source](#11: Behance Founder, Scott Belsky, on maximizing creative output and the role constraints play))

**Implication:** Don't over-capitalize your team or your projects. Funding that removes all constraints often removes the creative pressure that produces the best work. Consider keeping budget tighter than you need to, hiring one fewer person than feels comfortable, and using the constraint as a forcing function for finding genuinely novel solutions.

**Conviction is the most scarce resource in the messy middle — not money, not talent, not market timing.** When everything feels uncertain and the evidence is ambiguous, the team looks to the leader to model the belief that the thing is worth finishing. Leaders who communicate confidence performatively but privately doubt the mission create a credibility gap that people sense immediately. Genuine conviction — the kind that persists through real doubt — is what sustains teams through the grinding plateau. ([source](Navigating the Messy Middle - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Work on your own genuine belief before you try to inspire others. If you have lost faith in the mission, the team will know before you tell them. Rebuilding authentic conviction — by revisiting the original problem, talking to customers who need you, or articulating the future you're building — is leadership work, not motivational theater.

**The relationship between creativity and productivity inverts in the messy middle compared to the early phase.** At the start, creativity and productivity feel synonymous — every idea generated feels like progress. In the middle, they often conflict: more ideas create more optionality but less forward momentum. The most productive thing a creative team can do in the messy middle is frequently to stop generating new ideas and commit fully to executing the ones already in motion. ([source](The Founder List: Scott Belsky (Founder of Behance) on 'Creativity is The New Productivity'))

**Implication:** Implement explicit 'idea moratoriums' during critical execution phases. Signal clearly to the team that this is not a time for new input — it's a time for output. The creative instinct to keep exploring is healthy and necessary, but it must be channeled into improving execution on existing commitments rather than generating new starting points.

**The best founders develop what Belsky calls an 'immunity to embarrassment' in the messy middle — the ability to show work that isn't finished, share ideas that are half-formed, and ask for help without the identity threat that stops most people. This willingness to be publicly imperfect creates compounding advantages: faster feedback loops, stronger investor and advisor relationships, and a team culture that tolerates the necessary messiness of genuine creation.** ([source](TEDxPugetSound - Scott Belsky - Making Ideas Happen))

**Implication:** Practice showing work earlier and more vulnerably than feels comfortable. The embarrassment of sharing something unfinished is almost always smaller than the cost of building in isolation. Create internal sharing rituals — weekly demos, 'works in progress' reviews — that normalize imperfection as a stage of the creative process rather than a personal failure.

**'The Messy Middle' (2018) is Belsky's second national bestselling book, subtitled 'Finding Your Way Through the Hardest and Most Crucial Part of Any Bold Venture.' The book focuses on the unglamorous stretch between a venture's exciting launch and its ultimate success or failure — a phase that receives little attention compared to origin stories or exits. Belsky argues this middle phase is where most ventures are won or lost.** ([source](Wikipedia: Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Founders and leaders should prepare mentally and operationally for the messy middle of their ventures — the period of doubt, iteration, and grinding that follows initial momentum but precedes breakthrough.

**Scott authored 'The Messy Middle,' published in 2018, which focuses on the difficult period between starting and finishing any significant endeavor. This book directly addresses the under-discussed phase of building — not the glamorous launch or the triumphant exit, but the grind in between.** ([source](Co-op Careers Profile))

**Implication:** The messy middle of any venture — where most failures happen — is the least romanticized but most critical phase. Leaders and founders need frameworks for sustaining momentum and making decisions when the path is unclear.

---

## AI Agents & Agentic Commerce

**The 'memory layer' — a portable, permission-based system that allows AI agents to know and remember you across digital experiences — will become the new infrastructure for personalized commerce, functioning as a next-generation cookie that travels with the user across contexts.** ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"There are companies that are building what I call the memory layer, that allows memory to be portable across different agent experiences... imagine going to a digital experience and giving it permission to know you and then boom, everything's synced."*

**Implication:** The company that owns the portable memory layer controls the personalization stack — this is a critical infrastructure play that sits above the application layer and below the agent layer.

**AI enables new forms of social proxies — simulated versions of yourself that can engage with others asynchronously.** A boss's AI proxy could answer team questions without the boss being present; a person's AI proxy could have preliminary dating conversations before a real meeting. ([source](youtube:generative-now-steve-jang-scott-belsky))

> *"I wonder like how often do you want to ask your boss something in a job and you like don't want to ask them, but if like there was a proxy to your point, Steve, of them that was like very approachable... and maybe at the end of the conversation, you could even get the option of being like, can your boss see what just happened or do you want to hide it?"*

**Implication:** AI proxies could eliminate friction in high-stakes social and professional interactions — creating a new category of 'rehearsal layer' before real human engagement.

**Belsky reasons in implication chains.** if agents transact autonomously on behalf of consumers, then the economics of discovery, conversion, and loyalty all shift to whoever controls the agent. Search advertising, affiliate marketing, and the entire performance marketing stack were built for a world where humans browse and click — a world that agentic commerce structurally eliminates. ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** The entire digital advertising and affiliate ecosystem faces existential pressure from agentic commerce. Investors and operators in discovery-dependent businesses should model what their unit economics look like when agents shortcut the discovery layer entirely.

**Belsky notes that the seat-based SaaS pricing model collapses in an agentic world.** if an AI agent is doing the work that a human employee or contractor previously did using a software seat, the per-seat revenue model becomes economically incoherent. The shift to outcome-based or consumption-based pricing is not optional — it is an inevitable structural response to agent-driven workloads. ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** SaaS founders should begin modeling outcome-based and consumption-based pricing now, before agentic workloads make the transition forced rather than strategic. Companies that control their own pricing transition will capture more value than those who wait for market pressure to force it.

**Belsky warns that the proliferation of autonomous agents creates a new category of security and trust surface: if an agent can transact on a consumer's behalf, it can also be manipulated, spoofed, or captured by adversarial actors who understand how to influence agent behavior. The security architecture of the agentic layer is a first-order problem, not an afterthought.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Security, verification, and adversarial robustness need to be designed into agent infrastructure from day one — not retrofitted after incidents occur. Founders building in the agent layer should treat trust architecture with the same priority as product architecture.

**Belsky anticipates three sequential waves of agentic commerce.** first, agents that research and recommend on our behalf; second, agents that transact autonomously within defined parameters; and third, agents that develop genuine preferences and loyalty patterns independent of human instruction. Each wave compresses human decision-making further out of the loop, fundamentally restructuring who — or what — brands must win over to make a sale. ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Builders and brand strategists need to start designing for agent-legibility now — before wave two arrives. If your product's value proposition cannot be communicated to an AI agent evaluating it on a consumer's behalf, you risk invisibility in the next commerce layer.

**The most consequential business question of the agentic era is not who builds the best AI model, but who owns the agent layer — the layer that sits between a consumer's intent and the marketplace of options. Whoever controls that intermediary position controls the relationship, the data, and ultimately the economics of commerce. The agent layer is the new storefront.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Platform founders and investors should evaluate every consumer-facing AI product through the lens of agent-layer ownership. The defensible position is not the underlying model — it is the trusted agent that learns a user's preferences over time and acts on their behalf.

**Belsky argues that humans historically overestimated the value added by relationship-based intermediaries — brokers, advisors, agents — and tolerated significant friction and fees for what was essentially a human face on a largely transactional service. AI agents will expose this overvaluation rapidly, displacing intermediaries whose primary value was accessibility and rapport rather than genuine expertise or judgment.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Any business whose core value proposition is 'we make it easy to navigate complexity' rather than 'we provide irreplaceable expertise' is acutely vulnerable to agent displacement. Now is the time to ruthlessly audit whether your moat is relationship or knowledge.

**Belsky draws an analogy between the agentic commerce future and the pre-industrial village shopkeeper who knew every customer personally — their family size, preferences, budget, and history — and curated recommendations accordingly. AI agents restore that intimacy at scale, offering a hyper-personalized commerce experience that mass retail destroyed and that no human workforce could economically resurrect.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** The competitive advantage in agentic commerce belongs to whoever accumulates the richest longitudinal preference data per user. Depth of personal context — not breadth of inventory — becomes the primary moat. Build systems that learn, remember, and anticipate.

**In Belsky's view, the interface layer is where value concentrates in every major technology transition — and the agentic era is no exception. AI models themselves are rapidly commoditizing, but the trusted interface through which a user delegates decision-making to an agent is extraordinarily sticky. Whoever earns that delegation earns the consumer relationship for everything downstream.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** Don't compete on model performance — compete on trust and delegation UX. The product that gets a user to say 'just handle it for me' and mean it has won a relationship that is nearly impossible to displace once established.

**Belsky observes that consumer AI has so far delivered its greatest value to builders and developers — people who can operationalize AI's raw capabilities — while everyday consumers have seen only incremental improvements to their actual experience. The true consumer AI revolution, including the arrival of genuinely useful personal agents, remains ahead of us rather than behind us.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** The consumer agent market is still wide open. Founders who can translate AI capability into genuine consumer utility — not just chatbot interfaces, but proactive agents that remove friction from daily life — are entering a relatively uncrowded space with massive upside.

**Belsky frames agentic AI as the transition from copilot to autonomous operator — a shift from AI that assists human decisions to AI that makes and executes decisions within delegated boundaries. This transition is not merely technical but psychological: it requires consumers to extend genuine trust to a non-human actor, which is a far higher bar than simply using a recommendation engine.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** Building consumer trust for autonomous action is the central design challenge of the agentic era. Products must earn delegation incrementally — starting with low-stakes tasks, demonstrating reliability, and gradually expanding the scope of autonomy as the trust account grows.

**Belsky anticipates that agentic commerce will produce a profound inversion in brand strategy.** instead of brands marketing directly to consumers, they will need to market to agents that act on consumers' behalf. This shifts the optimization target from human emotion and aspiration to agent-legible signals of quality, fit, and value — a fundamentally different communication problem. ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** CMOs and brand strategists need to begin developing 'agent marketing' capabilities now. Structured data, verifiable quality signals, and machine-readable preference alignment will matter more than emotional advertising in a world where agents make the first cut before humans ever see options.

**Belsky draws a distinction between agents that optimize within expressed preferences and agents that develop their own preference models based on behavioral inference. The latter — agents that learn what you actually want rather than what you say you want — represent a qualitatively different capability and a qualitatively different business opportunity, closer to a genuinely personalized intelligence than a sophisticated filter.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** The most defensible agent products will be those that build deep behavioral preference models over time, not those that simply execute user instructions more efficiently. Longitudinal data and behavioral inference are the real asset — the interface is just the access point.

**Belsky applies his 'interface controls value' principle directly to the agent layer.** just as the browser controlled the internet and the app store controlled mobile, the dominant personal agent platform will control the agentic internet. The race to own the personal agent layer is the defining platform battle of this technology generation — and it is still in its very early stages. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** For platform investors and founders, the personal agent layer deserves the same strategic attention that the browser and mobile OS commanded in their respective eras. The window to establish dominant positioning is open now and will close faster than most people expect.

**Belsky sees agentic commerce as a force that restores consumer sovereignty in an unexpected way.** instead of being manipulated by algorithmic feeds designed to maximize engagement and impulse purchases, consumers with well-calibrated agents will get outcomes aligned with their actual long-term interests. The agent, properly built, is an advocate for the consumer — not for the merchant or the platform. ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Products that position their agent as a genuine consumer advocate — structurally aligned with user outcomes rather than merchant revenue — have a compelling trust narrative and a potentially durable loyalty moat. Consumer alignment is a competitive differentiator, not just an ethical position.

**Belsky views the prompt interface as a transitional form — a necessary but temporary bridge between human-controlled computing and agent-driven computing. The prompt box requires users to articulate their needs explicitly, which is a fundamentally unnatural behavior. The mature agent interface will infer intent from context and history, making explicit prompting as archaic as typing commands into a terminal.** ([source](Adobe's Scott Belsky: Art & design in the age of AI | Rapid Response))

**Implication:** Builders investing heavily in prompt UI and prompt engineering as long-term product differentiators may be optimizing for a transitional moment. The durable investment is in behavioral context models and intent inference — the capabilities that make explicit prompting unnecessary.

**Belsky argues that the 'Do-Show-Explain' hierarchy — doing something for a user beats showing them how, which beats explaining why — is especially applicable to agent design. The best agents act proactively on inferred user intent rather than waiting to be asked. The failure mode of most current AI products is that they explain and show rather than simply doing, which keeps the cognitive burden on the human rather than the agent.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Evaluate every feature of your AI product through the Do-Show-Explain hierarchy. Any workflow where users are still explaining their needs in natural language or navigating options is an opportunity to push further toward proactive action. The ultimate agent UX is one where the user barely notices the agent working.

**Belsky anticipates that agentic commerce will resurrect the economics of curation as a premium service.** When agents can efficiently execute any transaction, the scarce resource becomes knowing which transactions are worth making — taste, judgment, and editorial perspective. Curators who can encode their preferences into agent-legible signals will command significant premiums in an attention-saturated marketplace. ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Curators, tastemakers, and editorial voices should be exploring how to build agent-compatible curation products — outputs that are not just human-readable but that can inform and train the preference models of personal agents. The curator's value becomes infrastructure in the agentic economy.

**Belsky sees the arrival of personal agents as the moment when consumer AI finally delivers on its original promise.** Rather than requiring consumers to engage with AI through chat interfaces or explicit queries, mature personal agents will operate in the background — monitoring, learning, and acting — making AI's value felt through outcomes rather than interactions. The best consumer AI is the AI you never consciously use. ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** The metric for consumer agent success is not engagement — it is outcomes achieved per unit of user attention. Products optimized for engagement will lose to products optimized for invisible effectiveness. Redesign your success metrics accordingly.

**Belsky reasons that as agents handle an increasing share of routine commerce and information tasks, the remaining human decision-making will concentrate in areas of genuine personal significance — identity, relationships, creative expression, and values-aligned consumption. Humans won't stop deciding; they will decide less frequently but more meaningfully, with agents handling the commodity layer of daily choices.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Products that serve the high-stakes, identity-connected decisions that humans will refuse to delegate to agents are in a structurally protected market. Build for the decisions people take personally — the choices they want to own — rather than the routine transactions they are happy to outsource.

**Belsky connects his 'small teams with AI beat large teams without it' thesis directly to the agentic era: a ten-person company with a well-deployed agent stack — handling commerce, customer service, research, and operations autonomously — can now match the throughput of organizations that previously required hundreds of employees. Revenue per employee is not just a vanity metric but the core signal of agentic leverage.** ([source](How To Do More With Less People - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** The benchmark for evaluating team efficiency is shifting from headcount productivity to agent leverage ratio — how much autonomous output your agent infrastructure generates relative to your human team. Organizations that measure and optimize this ratio will outcompete on unit economics by a significant margin.

**Belsky identifies a critical tension at the heart of agentic design.** agents that are maximally efficient tend to converge on a small set of optimized choices, which risks collapsing the diversity and serendipity that make consumer experience valuable and that keep markets competitive. If every agent optimizes for the same signals, commerce could narrow dangerously — fewer winners, less discovery, more concentration. ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Agent designers must actively engineer for serendipity and diversity of outcome, not just efficiency. Pure optimization without diversity constraints produces brittle, monopolistic commerce ecosystems. Building controlled randomness and exploratory behavior into agent logic is a design imperative, not a nice-to-have.

**Belsky positions the question of agent ownership — whether a consumer's personal agent is controlled by the consumer, by a platform, or by a merchant ecosystem — as the defining governance question of the agentic economy. A platform-controlled agent has structural incentives that diverge from the consumer's interests, creating the same conflicts of interest that plagued search advertising and social media algorithmic feeds.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Consumer-aligned agent products have a genuine opportunity to differentiate on governance — who the agent serves and who controls its incentive structure. Transparent, consumer-first agent architectures will become a marketing and trust differentiator as consumers grow more sophisticated about conflicts of interest in AI systems.

**Belsky sees the first mile of agent onboarding as a product design problem of exceptional importance: the moment a consumer first delegates a real task to an agent is the highest-leverage UX moment in the entire relationship. If the agent fails or frustrates in that first interaction, the consumer's willingness to delegate further collapses — perhaps permanently. Getting the first mile of agent trust right is the product priority above all others.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Apply the full first-mile discipline to agent onboarding: identify the single lowest-stakes, highest-certainty task where an agent can demonstrate flawless autonomous execution, and make that the first thing a new user experiences. Build trust from a successful small delegation before asking for meaningful delegation.

---

## Product Craft & the First Mile

**Technologists consistently run ahead of where customers actually want to be.** Personalized entertainment with different endings and audience cameos sounds compelling to builders but audiences fundamentally want shared experiences they can discuss together — divergent content breaks the social function of storytelling. ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"I know technologists that are obsessed with personalized entertainment and a film starring the audience has cameos and people having different endings, and I don't see evidence that audiences want that. I think that audiences want a shared experience that they can all recollect and talk about at a dinner table together."*

**Implication:** Product teams should validate whether personalization serves human social needs or merely satisfies a builder's technological imagination — not all personalization creates value for end users.

**Belsky's cardinal thesis for consumer technology.** the best products take us back to how things once were — with more scale and efficiency. For nearly all of human history, commerce was intimate and personal. Great consumer tech restores that intimacy at scale rather than inventing something entirely new. ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"My cardinal thesis of technology that's great for consumers is that it takes us back to the way things once were that we long for with more scale and efficiency."*

**Implication:** Product designers and founders should ask not 'what's technically possible' but 'what human experience are people longing to recover' — the strongest consumer products are restorations, not inventions.

**The right compass for deploying new technology in creative work is to be at the service of the creator.** If creators want a technology to expand the surface area of their creative possibilities, use it. If they don't, don't. Technology for its own sake is not the goal. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"My compass is to be at the service of the creator. If no creator wants to have new technology to revisualize and explore the full surface area of possibility of their character development, then we shouldn't use new technology for them. But if they do want that, I think my job and all of our jobs as makers of tools is to be at the service of the creator and help them achieve whatever is in their mind's eye."*

**Implication:** Tool makers who center creator agency rather than technological capability earn lasting trust — the question is never 'what can the technology do' but 'what does the creator need to express their vision.'

**Founders often build with passion for a solution rather than genuine empathy for the customer's problem.** Passion gets you started and gets people to join, but without continuously recalibrating empathy for the actual friction customers experience, products drift away from real need. ([source](youtube:bN7E44j7Xl8))

> *"They are founded by people who are deeply passionate about a solution to a problem but aren't necessarily empathetic with the customers suffering from that problem... You can never spend enough time with the customers, making sure, and rechecking, and recalibrating that empathy with what the problem is you're trying to solve."*

**Implication:** Empathy is not a one-time discovery exercise at the start of a venture — it requires ongoing, active maintenance throughout the entire product development lifecycle.

**Visual and interactive communication is replacing text-based communication in professional settings — product managers and executives are using prototype and experience design tools to 'show versus tell,' making conversations more focused and productive through interactive models.** ([source](nfx_founder_list:scott_belsky_creativity_productivity))

> *"Already we're seeing product managers and executives using prototype tools like Adobe XD and other experience design tools to show versus tell their ideas, creating interactive models that have the ability to make conversations focused and productive."*

**Implication:** The ability to prototype and visualize ideas is becoming a core professional competency beyond design roles — tools that enable rapid visual communication for non-designers are capturing enterprise value.

**Design is about the little things that make a big difference.** Small details — like professional photography for Airbnb listings or subtle UX decisions — are routinely deprioritized by both large corporations and early-stage startups, yet these decisions often determine whether a product stands out. ([source](youtube:highresolution_ep11))

> *"Design is very much the little things that make a big difference. I think that little things tend to be overridden in big corporations and sometimes deprioritized in small new teams and yet some of these little things like whether it's the aesthetic in some part of the user experience of a product whether it's the decision to professionally photograph apartments for Airbnb — these little decisions end up making a big difference."*

**Implication:** Founders and product leaders who defer design details as 'we'll get to that later' are systematically underinvesting in their core competitive advantage.

**Companies that outsource design at any stage are implicitly declaring that design is not their competitive advantage.** The strongest design-driven companies — Airbnb, Warby Parker, Uber, Pinterest — either co-founded with designers or embedded deep design conviction from the very start. ([source](youtube:highresolution_ep11))

**Implication:** Investors and founders should treat design outsourcing as a structural signal that the team does not believe design will determine their outcome — which in most consumer and product markets is a dangerous bet.

**A mockup is more persuasive than hours of debate.** In cross-functional meetings, showing a concrete visual representation immediately aligns stakeholders in ways that memos, presentations, and verbal arguments cannot. Designers should assert the mockup as the primary communication medium. ([source](youtube:highresolution_ep11))

**Implication:** Designers who wait to be invited to show their work are ceding influence; they should proactively insert visual artifacts into discussions to accelerate alignment and lead the conversation.

**When presenting design work, don't just display a mockup — frame it as a problem-solving artifact.** Invite stakeholders to surface remaining problems rather than give design direction. The designer's goal is to redirect 'make the logo bigger' feedback into 'what problem do you still feel isn't solved?' ([source](youtube:highresolution_ep11))

> *"You put up a mock and you say this is a concept, what questions do you have, and this is the problem that you asked us to solve. Now start peppering me with questions. When people start to say things like 'make the logo bigger' a great designer asks the people to rephrase it as problems they think still exist."*

**Implication:** Reframing design reviews as problem-identification sessions rather than aesthetic critique sessions gives designers control of the conversation and produces more actionable, useful feedback.

**Creative teams have switching benefits, not switching costs, when it comes to productivity tools.** Moving to a new tool often re-engages a creative team the way spring cleaning refreshes a space — which is why no single project management platform will ever dominate the creative market. ([source](youtube:AskGaryVee290))

> *"Most people talk about switching costs like investors like Gary and I will look and say, 'Oh, is there a high switching cost to this product,' and we'll retain its people. The funny thing about the creative world is there's actually switching benefits. When a creative team kind of stops using a tool, it's almost like spring cleaning."*

**Implication:** For product builders targeting creative professionals, retention strategy cannot rely on lock-in mechanics — it must rely on ongoing aesthetic and functional delight, because creatives will leave for novelty even when the current tool works.

**Creatives are drawn to visual elegance and aesthetic attraction in the tools they use — attraction breeds loyalty over time. Managing creative teams with spreadsheets or visually unappealing systems creates structural friction that undermines adoption regardless of functional utility.** ([source](youtube:AskGaryVee290))

> *"For a creative team, you have to start paying attention to the elegance of the system. Creatives are, we want different colored folders, we want to have something that we're visually attracted to. Attraction breeds loyalty over time."*

**Implication:** When building tools for creative professionals, visual design is not a nice-to-have — it is the primary adoption mechanism, and investing in aesthetic quality is directly equivalent to investing in retention.

**The first mile of a product experience — the first 30 seconds — is where users are lazy, vain, and selfish.** They want quick success, want to look good, and refuse to tolerate any learning curve. Most teams spend the final mile of their time building the product considering the first mile of customer experience, which is a critical misallocation of energy. ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"In the first 30 seconds of using a new product, you are lazy, vain, and selfish. You want to get it done super quickly. You want to look good to your colleagues or to your friends. You want to feel successful very quickly by engaging in this product. You don't want to have to watch a tour, read anything, really endure any learning curve whatsoever."*

**Implication:** Product teams should disproportionately invest in onboarding, orientation, and defaults — the top of funnel — before adding features, because getting users through that first moment is the highest-leverage activity in product development.

**Product sense is grounded in empathy, not passion.** Teams fall in love with solutions before truly understanding the problem — and that passion can point them 30 degrees off from what the customer actually needs. The discipline of developing genuine empathy for customers' lived context is what surfaces the right solution. ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"The biggest mistakes that teams make is they become very passionate about a solution to a problem they're trying to solve as opposed to doing everything they can to develop empathy for the customer that's suffering the problem. And often times the empathy gives you the solution, whereas the passion you have for whatever you think the solution is might be 30° off with what the solution actually is."*

**Implication:** PMs and founders should invest heavily in customer observation — not just usage data — to ensure their conviction is rooted in the customer's reality rather than their own enthusiasm for a particular solution.

**True customer empathy requires observing users in the full context of their day — not just while using your product.** Users experience your product amid meetings, family demands, competing apps, and cognitive overload. That surrounding context is the data you're missing when you only watch product usage. ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"The most humbling moments for me as a product leader have always been shoulder to shoulder to customers watching them actually go about their day. Not just use my product, but go about their day. Because what you end up getting is context for a lot of data that you're missing."*

**Implication:** Contextual observation — not just usability testing — is the most powerful tool for building genuine product empathy and intuition, because it reveals the true competitive environment your product lives in.

**The first mile of product experience must be continually reimagined because user cohorts change.** Early adopters are forgiving; pragmatist customers who join later are more skeptical and less tolerant of friction. What worked for cohort one will fail cohort three — product-market fit is never finished. ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"Every new cohort of new customers is different. The new customers you have in the early stages of your product are typically more willing and forgiving customers... now you're engaging more of those pragmatist customers, those later stage customers who are initially more skeptical, less forgiving, less willing to deal with your friction. And so you have to reimagine the onboarding process all over again."*

**Implication:** Teams should treat the first mile as a living, evolving system tied to which customer segment is entering the funnel — not a solved problem that gets set and forgotten.

**Great products are talked about not because they do what's expected, but because they do what's unexpected.** Surprise and delight — the unanticipated Easter egg, the delightful discovery — are what generate word-of-mouth and emotional attachment. Most product teams don't explicitly design for unexpectedness. ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"People don't talk about a product doing exactly what they expected it to do. They talk about a product doing what they didn't expect. And so it always is interesting to me — why aren't we optimizing for those things that people wouldn't expect the product to do as a way to get that surprise and delight, to talk about it, to develop a relationship with our products?"*

**Implication:** Building in deliberate moments of surprise — features or interactions users didn't expect but love — is a high-leverage growth strategy that generates organic conversation and emotional loyalty.

**The discipline of building half — half the features, half the options, half the target market — is counterintuitive but essential. When you concentrate the product on one core metric and eliminate everything else, the core crank operates at dramatically higher velocity. Complexity dilutes focus and suppresses the very behavior you want to drive.** ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"If you make the whole product about one thing, everyone does that, that core crank operates at like 10x the velocity, and if that's the most important metric for the business, that's gold. And so we basically went on a killing spree. We just started killing things."*

**Implication:** Product teams should regularly audit what can be removed, not just what can be added — and use core metric performance as the empirical test of whether pruning is working.

**When deciding what to build first in an MVP, teams should optimize for the problems they want to have — not problems they're afraid of. The goal is getting users to a point where they care enough to demand more features. Until users reach that level of engagement, adding features is premature.** ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"Whenever I'm asked by teams what features need to be part of their MVP — I always tell them to optimize for the problems they want to have. You want the problem of customers getting through your funnel, feeling successful, using your product and getting value, and then saying to you, 'Oh, but I need it on this platform, or I need this capability.' You want those problems. So don't do those features now."*

**Implication:** MVP design should be an exercise in removing every obstacle to the first moment of customer success — not an exercise in adding capabilities to satisfy imagined future needs.

**Experienced product thinkers have a 'golden gut' — the instinct to reduce cognitive load even when it means removing features that some users value, because the aggregate benefit of a simpler flow for the majority outweighs the cost of disappointing a minority. This micro-decision-making capacity separates junior from senior product thinkers.** ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"Experienced product thinkers with that golden gut of 'Oh my gosh, wait. Reduction of cognitive load. Maybe if even 10% of people get confused, to get 90% of people far faster through this process is a big win and a great opportunity cost trade-off.' Those sorts of little micro decisions that we make in the process of building products — that's the golden gut."*

**Implication:** Product leaders should cultivate and protect the judgment to make asymmetric trade-offs in favor of simplicity — even when vocal minorities push back — and use empirical data to validate those instincts.

**Removing a feature triggers loud short-term resistance that almost always fades within 24-48 hours — and the underlying product metric typically improves. The noise of user complaints about removals is systematically louder than the signal, which causes teams to over-preserve features they should kill.** ([source](youtube:HCKosdV1J-8))

> *"We took it away. For 24 hours, we had people reaching out to us being like, 'Damn you. How could you take away these controls for color in your portfolio?' After the 24 hours, we basically never heard about it again. All the portfolios looked cleaner and more consistent, and people did the core metric more."*

**Implication:** Teams should not let the loudness of the initial protest prevent good product decisions — the practical test is what happens to core metrics after the dust settles, not whether users complain in the moment.

**The pragmatic, responsible majority of creative customers — not the 'burn the boats' accelerationists or the 'don't do AI' resisters — should be the anchor for product and ethical decisions. This group is also the largest and most commercially significant customer segment.** ([source](youtube:adobe-scott-belsky-rapid-response))

> *"The average is a large group in the middle that are very pragmatic and responsible about this technology... those are the folks that we're taking the pulse of constantly and anchoring ourselves to, and that also feeds the business — it's the biggest customer base too."*

**Implication:** Product teams should resist being pulled by vocal extremes in either direction; the silent pragmatic majority represents both the ethical center and the largest revenue opportunity.

**The 10-star design framework — imagining the absolute best possible experience and then working backward to what is actually achievable and scalable — is a powerful method for discovering the upper bound of product experience and finding the highest-fidelity version that can be delivered at scale.** ([source](youtube:chase-jarvis-live-reid-hoffman))

**Implication:** Product teams should define the ceiling of possible experience before designing the floor of minimum viable experience. This prevents anchoring too low and reveals achievable moments of delight.

**Focus groups tell you what people want, not what they need.** Behance's only focus group revealed that creatives didn't want another social network — but when asked what problems they had, they described exactly what a network could solve: attribution, discovery, portfolio management. The insight was to build what they need, not what they say they want. ([source](youtube:SXSW_Interactive_2013_Belsky))

> *"We had to take that leap of recognizing that we're not really building what they want but what they need."*

**Implication:** Product builders should use qualitative research to surface pain points and frustrations, not to validate features — users describe problems better than they prescribe solutions.

**Modern enterprise software buyers demand measurable value within 3–6 months of deployment.** The era of 18-month implementation timelines that might work is over — time-to-value has compressed to become a buying criterion itself, reshaping product design and go-to-market strategy. ([source](greycroft_mark_terbeek_outliers))

> *"They want to be able to do it with a specific short-term time to value in mind where it's not this like 18 months and hope that it works kind of type implementation it's got to be clear value in three months six months at the most."*

**Implication:** Enterprise software products must be architected for fast onboarding and early wins — the first 90 days of customer experience is now as important as long-term functionality in determining renewal and expansion.

**Connecting mobile apps directly to community — enabling publishing work-in-progress from mobile to a portfolio platform — reduces the friction between creation and sharing to near zero. Lower friction at the point of sharing leads to earlier and more frequent uploads.** ([source](youtube:adobe_create_now_uk_behance))

> *"The creative profile opens up all kinds of possibilities for integrating behance into creative workflows like quickly publishing work in progress from your desktop and mobile apps adding portfolio projects directly from many of the mobile apps."*

**Implication:** Product teams should think of the share/publish action as a core feature to optimize — every reduction in friction between creation and community sharing increases the volume and velocity of content that feeds the platform's network effects.

**Adobe's approach to generative AI is creator-centric — building capabilities that amplify what creative professionals can do rather than bypassing them. The reception to Generative Fill in Photoshop confirmed that professionals embrace AI when it genuinely extends their capabilities.** ([source](adobe_live:max_london_belsky_interview))

> *"our team took a very creator-centric approach in bringing some of these capabilities into Photoshop... I think the reception has certainly exceeded our imagination — what people are doing with it, how they're changing their process to incorporate it."*

**Implication:** Products that frame AI as professional empowerment rather than replacement earn faster adoption and deeper loyalty from skilled user bases — the framing of the value proposition matters as much as the capability itself.

**Belsky notes that the prompt interface — the blank text box that dominated early generative AI products — is itself a first-mile failure for most users. Most people don't know what to ask for, feel exposed by a blank page, and produce mediocre results without knowing how to improve them. The next generation of AI creative tools will replace the prompt box with more intuitive, tactile, direct-manipulation interfaces that guide users to great outcomes without requiring them to master prompt engineering.** ([source](Adobe's Scott Belsky: Art & design in the age of AI | Rapid Response))

**Implication:** If you're building AI-powered products, treat the prompt box as a transitional interface and invest in discovering what replaces it for your specific user. The teams who solve the first-mile problem for AI tools — making them feel as intuitive as the best non-AI products — will capture the mainstream market that current AI tools consistently fail to convert.

**Belsky frames the first-mile problem through a simple but powerful hierarchy.** doing something proactively for the user beats showing them how to do it, which in turn beats explaining why they should. Most products over-invest in explanation — onboarding tutorials, tooltip walkthroughs, and help documentation — while under-investing in just doing the work on behalf of the user so value is felt immediately without any cognitive load. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Before writing another line of onboarding copy or adding a product tour, ask whether the feature could simply be pre-configured, pre-populated, or pre-executed for the user. The ideal onboarding experience is one where the user never needs to be taught anything.

**Radical simplification is not a design preference — it is a strategic discipline.** Belsky contends that the willingness to remove features, reduce options, and narrow scope is what separates enduring products from ones that collapse under their own complexity. The hardest product decisions are not what to build but what to cut, especially when the thing being cut was built with care and has existing users. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Build a recurring practice of subtraction into your product process — not just sprint reviews for what to add next, but deliberate sessions focused on what to remove. The products that compound in value over time are almost always the ones that got more focused, not more comprehensive.

**Belsky argues that product teams consistently underestimate the emotional and psychological state of a brand-new user.** That person arrives with no context, limited patience, competing distractions, and deep skepticism about whether this product is worth their time. Designing for this person — rather than for an informed, motivated user who already understands the value proposition — is the fundamental discipline of first-mile product design. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Regularly stress-test your product with genuinely cold users who have zero context or prior exposure. The gap between how your team experiences the product and how a cold user experiences it is the first-mile problem made visible — and most teams are shocked by how wide that gap is.

**Belsky's concept of the first mile extends beyond onboarding screens to encompass the entire first episode of value a user has with a product — from how they discover it, to their first interaction, to the first time they get a result they care about. Each step in this journey is a potential dropout point, and the cumulative drop-off across these steps means that a far smaller percentage of people who encounter a product ever experience what makes it truly great.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Map the full first-mile funnel from discovery to first meaningful outcome, and measure the dropout rate at each step. Most teams know their signup conversion but not their 'first value' conversion. The gap between those two numbers is the first-mile problem quantified.

**Belsky observes that AI is fundamentally changing the first-mile problem by enabling products to be deeply personalized before the user has ever done anything. An AI-powered product can infer context, adapt its interface, and pre-configure its experience based on signals that previously required extensive user input. This shifts the first-mile opportunity from 'how do we teach the user quickly' to 'how do we already know what the user needs.'** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Teams building with AI should reimagine onboarding from scratch rather than applying AI as a layer on top of existing flows. The question is no longer how to walk users through setup — it's how to eliminate setup entirely by letting the product infer and adapt from the first interaction.

**Belsky argues that product teams should be deeply suspicious of their own engagement metrics as proxies for genuine user value. High engagement can reflect compulsion, confusion, or inefficiency as easily as it reflects delight. The question is not whether users are spending time in the product but whether they are achieving outcomes they care about and whether the product is making them feel capable and successful.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Add outcome-based metrics alongside engagement metrics in your product dashboards. Measure whether users achieved what they came to do, not just whether they spent time doing it. The products that earn long-term loyalty are the ones that respect users' time by making them faster and more capable, not the ones that maximize time-on-site.

**Belsky argues that taste is itself a product design input, not just an aesthetic preference.** The decisions that define a product's character — what to include, how to sequence interactions, what friction to preserve versus eliminate, what to make prominent versus buried — are acts of editorial judgment that require developed taste. Teams that lack taste produce products that are technically functional but emotionally inert. ([source](Where Scott Belsky Got His Good Taste))

**Implication:** Invest in the taste development of your product team the same way you invest in their technical skills. This means exposing them to great products across categories, building a shared vocabulary for what makes experiences feel right, and creating a culture where aesthetic judgment is treated as a legitimate form of product expertise.

**Belsky draws a direct line between product simplicity and the ability to tell a compelling story about a product.** Products that have drifted into complexity become impossible to explain simply, which makes them harder to sell, harder to market, and harder for existing users to evangelize to others. Simplicity is not just a UX virtue — it is a business development and growth asset that compounds over time. ([source](Building Adobe & the Future of Design with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Use the one-sentence product explanation as a diagnostic tool. If your team cannot agree on a single clear sentence that describes what your product does and for whom, the product itself has likely accumulated too much complexity. Simplifying the product often makes the story clear, not the reverse.

**Belsky points out that the creative tools category — which he has studied and worked within deeply throughout his career at Adobe — faces a particular version of the first-mile problem: creative tools are often powerful but intimidating, and the gap between what the tool can do and what a new user can do with it in the first session is enormous. Bridging that gap is not just a product nicety but a business imperative, because users who don't create something they're proud of in the first session almost never return.** ([source](Building Adobe & the Future of Design with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** For any product where output quality matters to the user, design the first-session experience around guaranteeing a high-quality output — even if that means heavily constraining the user's choices in that first session. Let them produce something they're proud to share before you expose them to the full power of the tool.

**The first mile of a product — particularly the first 30 seconds — determines whether users continue or abandon.** In this window, users behave according to three predictable traits: they are lazy (unwilling to invest effort to understand), vain (concerned about how they appear to others), and selfish (demanding immediate personal return before committing). Belsky argues that more than 30% of total product energy should be allocated to this narrow window, because getting it wrong makes everything else irrelevant. ([source](The Messy Middle (book) / First Round Review: 'How to Shape Remarkable Products in the Messy Middle'))

**Implication:** Teams should audit where their design and engineering investment actually goes. If less than a third of effort is going to first-run experience and onboarding, the allocation is almost certainly wrong — no matter how sophisticated the core product is.

**Belsky argues that the first hook in a product experience must connect a user's immediate, short-term self-interest to a longer-term promise. Users are not going to project forward and imagine future benefits — they need to feel value in the moment, anchored to something they already care about. The hook is not a marketing message; it is a product design problem that has to be solved in the actual experience.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book)))

**Implication:** Map out what a new user wants in the first 30 seconds — not what your product does, but what that specific user cares about right now. Design the first interaction around delivering precisely that, and let the broader value proposition emerge from experience rather than explanation.

**Belsky contends that most products fail not because they lack features but because they fail to make users feel capable and successful immediately. The vanity dimension of the lazy-vain-selfish framework is underappreciated: users care deeply about how using a product makes them look to others, and a product that makes someone feel stupid or incompetent in its first moments will be abandoned regardless of its power.** ([source](The Messy Middle (book) / First Round Review: 'How to Shape Remarkable Products in the Messy Middle of Building Startups'))

**Implication:** Design the first experience to make users feel competent and even impressive. Early wins that users can share or reference — output they're proud of — are among the most effective retention and acquisition mechanisms available, and they're primarily a product design decision, not a marketing one.

**Belsky argues that a product's core differentiator should never be subject to MVP-style corner-cutting.** The minimum viable product framework is valuable for peripheral features and unproven hypotheses, but applying it to the thing that makes you different communicates low conviction to the market and trains early adopters to expect mediocrity. If something is your reason for existing, it deserves disproportionate craft and investment from day one. ([source](The Messy Middle (book)))

**Implication:** Be explicit about what is and is not eligible for MVP treatment on your roadmap. Peripheral features, integrations, and supporting workflows can be rough — but the core value proposition should launch with the highest craft your team can produce, even if that means launching later or smaller.

**The selfishness dimension of new user behavior — the expectation of immediate personal return before any investment — means that onboarding cannot ask for effort before delivering value.
