**Implication:** Having a clear, durable mission that transcends any single company or product gives leaders a consistent filter for decisions. Scott's through-line across very different roles shows the power of mission-driven career building.

**Scott authored 'Making Ideas Happen,' an international bestselling book about turning creative visions into reality, published in 2010 by Portfolio/Penguin. The book targets the gap between having ideas and executing them — a perennial challenge for creative professionals and entrepreneurs.** ([source](Co-op Careers Profile))

**Implication:** The execution gap — the distance between idea and reality — remains one of the most underserved problems for creative and entrepreneurial people. Products, frameworks, and communities that help close this gap have outsized market demand.

**Scott publishes 'Implications,' a widely-read industry newsletter sent to tens of thousands of product leaders, investors, designers, and other leaders every month. The newsletter focuses on implications of trends rather than the trends themselves — a distinct editorial lens. It demonstrates his ongoing commitment to thought leadership outside of institutional roles.** ([source](Co-op Careers Profile))

**Implication:** Building a direct audience through a newsletter is a powerful way for operators-turned-investors to maintain influence and intellectual relevance. The framing of 'implications' — not just news but meaning — is a differentiated editorial strategy worth emulating.

---

## Longevity & Health

**Belsky uses health AI as his primary personal use case — aggregating blood results, wearable data, and health history into a persistent AI notebook. His thesis is that longitudinal health data will be far more valuable in ten years than in the moment, making consistent data capture now a form of long-term health investment.** ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"The idea of having all of your lab results and your Whoop data and your, you know, any sort of ailment and whatever else — all in one notebook of AI chats — that is there for a perpetual memory of your health is just a treasure trove of opportunity down the line. My view on a lot of this data is that it will be more useful ten years from now, when it has this longitudinal relevance."*

**Implication:** Health AI platforms that prioritize longitudinal data retention and synthesis — rather than point-in-time insights — will deliver dramatically superior outcomes and user stickiness over time.

**Doctors who don't integrate patient-generated AI health data into their practice within a few years will be committing a form of malpractice. The volume and depth of consumer health data now available makes it impossible to practice high-quality medicine without it.** ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"Any doctor who's not encouraging and using that years or a few years from now is like, it's malpractice, right? Like how in 15 minutes could a doctor give you a comprehensive sense of what sorts of things might be happening if you have a problem without having access?"*

**Implication:** Healthcare providers and payers should accelerate integration of consumer health data — the competitive and liability gap between AI-integrated and traditional practitioners will become untenable.

**Belsky has noted that blood testing and biomarker tracking are transforming health from a reactive system into a proactive one. Historically, people only engaged with the healthcare system when something went wrong. The new generation of at-home and clinic-accessible blood panels gives individuals a continuous data stream about their own biology, enabling intervention before symptoms appear. This is a fundamental inversion of the medical model.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Products that surface biomarker data to consumers must invest heavily in the first-mile experience — translating complex lab results into actionable, emotionally resonant insights within seconds of delivery, or users will disengage.

**Belsky has pointed to wearables as a category that exemplifies the broader pattern of technology making invisible things visible. Heart rate variability, blood oxygen, sleep stages, and stress markers were always present in the body — humans simply lacked the instruments to observe them continuously. Wearables don't create new biology; they create new awareness, and awareness is the precondition for behavior change.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Wearable health products should obsess over the awareness-to-action pipeline. Surfacing data is table stakes; the product value lives in translating that awareness into a specific next action the user takes within the next hour.

**Belsky has observed that preventative scanning — full-body MRIs, coronary calcium scores, and similar diagnostics — is moving from the domain of elite athletes and wealthy executives into mainstream accessibility. This democratization mirrors patterns he has observed in creative tools: technology that was once available only to professionals with expensive infrastructure eventually reaches everyone. When it does, the behavior change at population scale is enormous.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** The companies that will win in preventative diagnostics are those that solve the access and affordability layer — not the imaging technology itself, which will commoditize. The moat is distribution, trust, and follow-through care coordination.

**Belsky has consistently identified the intermediary disruption thesis as one of the biggest forces reshaping industries — and healthcare is no exception. He has suggested that AI agents will increasingly displace the coordinator and advisor roles within healthcare navigation: the scheduler, the insurance interpreter, the referral manager. These roles add relationship value but not clinical expertise, making them structurally vulnerable to agent-layer replacement.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Healthcare startups should focus agent-based automation on the coordination and navigation layer first — this is where friction is highest, consumer pain is acute, and clinical liability is lowest. Displace the intermediary before disrupting the clinician.

**Belsky has argued that the real unlock for longevity technology is not any single tool but the integration layer — the ability to synthesize data from wearables, blood panels, sleep trackers, and behavioral inputs into a coherent, personalized picture of one's health trajectory. Today those data streams exist in silos. The company that aggregates them into a unified, actionable health intelligence layer will capture enormous value, regardless of which individual devices or tests it relies on.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** The longevity platform play is an integration and intelligence layer sitting above hardware and diagnostic commodities. Build toward aggregation early, even if you start with a single data type — the compounding value of the integrated picture is the real product.

**Belsky has observed that the longevity space is experiencing the classic pattern of early-adopter technology diffusion: a small group of deeply committed practitioners — often founders, athletes, and executives — using bleeding-edge tools that are expensive, complex, and time-consuming. This group functions as the test bed that proves out the behavior patterns and product requirements before mass-market versions become viable. The question for investors and builders is not whether mainstream longevity tech will arrive but when, and which early-adopter behaviors will translate.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Study the behavior of the most obsessive early longevity adopters — the daily blood panel trackers, the continuous glucose monitor users, the full-body scan enthusiasts — not to copy their product exactly but to understand which habits will survive simplification and which are artifacts of the early-adopter personality.

**Belsky has pointed to the convergence of AI and biology as one of the most significant capability expansions in human history — comparable in magnitude to the industrial revolution's expansion of physical capability or the internet's expansion of informational capability. The ability to model individual biological systems with AI, predict disease trajectories, and personalize interventions at the genetic and metabolic level represents a new frontier for what it means to be human.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Founders in the AI-biology intersection should think in decade-long arcs, not product cycles. The infrastructure being built today — biomarker databases, genomic training sets, metabolic models — will compound in value over years in ways that short-term product metrics cannot capture.

**Belsky frames the longevity movement as a natural extension of his broader Superhumanity thesis — the idea that humans are increasingly using technology to expand their capabilities beyond biological defaults. Just as AI augments cognitive performance, health wearables and preventative diagnostics augment physical and metabolic performance. The two movements are not separate trends but two faces of the same shift: humans refusing to accept the limitations they were born with.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Builders in health tech should position their products not as medical tools but as capability-expansion platforms — the framing that resonates with early adopters is self-optimization, not disease prevention.

**Belsky has articulated that the coming wave of AI health coaches represents one of the most consequential consumer AI applications — not because they are smarter than doctors, but because they are always present. The bottleneck in preventative health has never been knowledge; it has been the absence of continuous, personalized feedback loops. An AI coach that monitors your biology in real time and nudges behavior closes that gap in a way that a quarterly physician visit never could.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** The winning health AI product is not the one with the best medical knowledge base — it is the one with the best continuous engagement loop. Design for daily ritual, not episodic consultation.

**In his Superhumanity framework, Belsky places longevity technology alongside cognitive enhancement tools as evidence that the definition of 'normal human capability' is being renegotiated in real time. The generation growing up with continuous health monitoring will have a fundamentally different relationship to their own biology than previous generations — one characterized by agency rather than passivity. Accepting biological decline as inevitable will come to seem as strange as accepting ignorance in the age of the internet.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Founders building in longevity should expect a generational adoption curve — younger users will onboard as capability-seekers, older users as risk-reducers. Product positioning and UX should be designed separately for each cohort.

**Belsky has consistently argued that the most valuable consumer AI experiences will be hyper-personalized — and health is the domain where personalization has the highest stakes. No two people's metabolic profiles, sleep architecture, or stress responses are identical. Generic health advice is structurally limited. AI systems that learn individual biology over time and tailor interventions accordingly represent a qualitatively different category of health tool, not merely an incremental improvement.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** The moat in AI health is longitudinal personal data, not algorithms. Build products that incentivize users to stay long enough to generate the data that makes the experience genuinely personalized — and protect that data as your core competitive asset.

**Belsky has tied the longevity movement to his conviction that humans will increasingly refuse to accept default settings — whether biological, cognitive, or social. The same impulse that drives a founder to reject the assumption that a market is unsolvable drives the longevity-focused individual to reject the assumption that aging is simply what happens. In both cases, the naive belief that things can be different is the engine of change.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Marketing longevity products to founders, creators, and leaders as an expression of the same contrarian agency mindset they bring to their work will resonate far more deeply than clinical or fear-based messaging.

**Belsky has noted that AI health coaching has the potential to be the first consumer AI application that materially changes outcomes at population scale — not because AI doctors are better than human doctors, but because most health decisions happen outside the clinic, in moments of temptation, fatigue, or stress. An AI system embedded in those moments with personalized context can influence behavior where human healthcare professionals structurally cannot.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** The battleground for AI health coaches is not the clinic interface — it is the notification, the wearable nudge, and the ambient check-in. Invest product resources in micro-moment interventions rather than dashboard comprehensiveness.

**Within his Superhumanity framework, Belsky draws a parallel between cognitive tools — note-taking apps, AI writing assistants, memory augmentation — and physical health tools like continuous glucose monitors and HRV trackers. Both categories function as exocortex or exobody extensions: devices that make the human system more legible to itself. The unifying theme is that self-knowledge, whether cognitive or physiological, is the foundation of self-improvement.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Product teams in health and productivity should study each other's onboarding and engagement patterns — the psychological levers that make someone return daily to a fitness tracker are closely related to those that drive daily use of a knowledge management tool.

**Belsky has framed the longevity and health optimization movement as part of a broader cultural shift in which the most ambitious and high-performing individuals treat their body as infrastructure, not just as a vehicle. In the same way that a serious creative professional invests in the best tools, a serious performer invests in sleep quality, metabolic health, and stress resilience. The health-as-infrastructure mindset is spreading from elite athletes and biohackers into the mainstream knowledge-worker population.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Health optimization products targeting knowledge workers, founders, and creatives should be positioned as professional infrastructure investments, not wellness luxuries. Pricing and positioning should reflect the ROI frame: better biology produces better cognitive output.

**Belsky has noted that the Superhumanity movement — of which longevity is a component — is fundamentally optimistic in its anthropology. It bets that humans want more agency, more capability, and more lifespan, and that they will adopt tools enabling this at scale once friction drops below a threshold. This is a contrarian position relative to the 'humans are inherently resistant to behavior change' assumption that has frustrated digital health for two decades.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Design longevity products around the assumption of high user motivation and agency — not around the assumption that users need to be tricked or incentivized into engagement. Gamification and nudging are proxies for poor product-market fit; real agency requires no bribing.

**Belsky has consistently applied his 'interface controls value' principle to the health technology stack.** The underlying biology sensors, genomic sequencers, and imaging hardware will commoditize over time. The entity that controls the interface through which individuals understand and act on their health data will capture the majority of the value — just as Apple captured smartphone value not by building the best radio chips but by owning the interface layer through which all device value was experienced. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** In health tech, the interface layer is the longitudinal health dashboard or AI coach that users return to daily. Build toward owning that layer — even if you start by aggregating other companies' sensor data — because interface ownership is where lasting competitive moats are built.

**Belsky has argued that one of the most underappreciated aspects of the longevity movement is its psychological dimension. The shift from passively accepting biological decline to actively monitoring and intervening in one's health trajectory requires a fundamental change in identity — from patient to agent. Products that facilitate this identity shift, not just the behavioral change, will see dramatically higher retention and advocacy than those focused purely on health outcomes.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Invest in the identity and community layer of longevity products, not just the data and intervention layer. Users who identify as active agents of their own biology — not just consumers of health data — will build the kind of deep product attachment that drives word-of-mouth and low churn.

**Belsky has connected the longevity movement to his broader thesis that consumer AI has so far underdelivered on its promise for everyday people. Health is one of the domains where genuinely personalized AI has the highest potential to create visible, life-changing value — and therefore one of the most likely places where the consumer AI revolution will finally become tangible to non-technical users. The first AI product that materially extends a mainstream user's healthy lifespan will do more to legitimize consumer AI than any chatbot.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** Consumer AI health is not a feature — it is the beachhead for proving that AI creates real value in everyday life. Build health AI with the ambition of being the product that changes the mainstream narrative about what AI can do for regular people.

**Belsky has suggested that the first-mile experience problem is especially acute in health technology.** When someone receives a blood test result, a wearable alert, or a scan finding, they are in a high-anxiety, low-context moment. Most health products overwhelm users with data at exactly that moment — violating the principle that the first impression must provide immediate, personalized value rather than requiring effort to interpret. Simplifying and humanizing the moment of data delivery is one of the most underinvested areas in health product design. ([source](The Messy Middle — Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Health product teams should apply the 'lazy, vain, selfish' user framework ruthlessly to the result-delivery experience. Assume users will not read the explanation, care deeply about what the result says about them personally, and want to know one specific next action — not a dashboard.

**Belsky has noted that the messy middle of building a longevity or health company is particularly brutal because the feedback loops between product intervention and measurable health outcome can span years. Unlike a consumer app where engagement metrics are visible within days, a preventative health product may not show outcome validation for years. This creates an especially long and psychologically demanding messy middle — requiring founders with unusual capacity for endurance and conviction in the absence of near-term validation.** ([source](The Messy Middle — Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Investors and founders in longevity health need to design their funding structures, team psychology, and milestone frameworks around the long feedback loop. Interim proxy metrics — engagement, behavior change, biomarker movement — must be identified upfront as substitutes for the long-horizon outcome data that won't be available for years.

**Belsky has connected his SXSW 2011 conversation with Team SHAE — an early AI health advisor concept — to a broader thesis about personalized health guidance as a long-standing unsolved problem. Even in 2011, he was interested in the idea of systems that could learn individual biology and deliver tailored recommendations. The fact that this vision is only now becoming technically feasible underscores how long the foundational ideas in consumer health AI have been gestating.** ([source](A Conversation with Scott Belsky and Team SHAE @ SXSW 2011))

**Implication:** The consumer AI health space is not a new idea finding a new moment — it is a long-held vision finally meeting sufficient technical capability. Founders entering now benefit from a decade of user expectation-setting; the market is primed for products that actually deliver on promises made years ago.

**Belsky has observed that the longevity and health optimization space will follow the same creative meritocracy dynamics he identified in the creative economy through Behance: the best outcomes will increasingly be visible, shareable, and attributable to specific protocols, tools, and practitioners. Just as Behance made creative talent legible and portable, health data platforms will eventually make biological outcomes legible — enabling practitioners, coaches, and protocols to earn reputation based on verified results rather than credentials alone.** ([source](TEDxGotham 2011 - Scott Belsky - Making a Creative Meritocracy))

**Implication:** Build reputation and outcome-verification layers into health platforms early. The credentialing system in healthcare is a legacy intermediary ripe for disruption by outcome-based reputation systems — and the platforms that build this infrastructure will define who is trusted in the next era of health guidance.

---

## Enterprise AI & Internal Tools

**AI will materially reduce waste across industries by optimizing resource utilization, supply chains, and procurement in ways previously managed by human intuition and Excel spreadsheets. This will compress margins across industries and simultaneously reduce environmental impact.** ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

> *"There's always this margin of stuff that is just, in some ways is still human managed. And we're in Excel spreadsheets. And now, as you have AI tackle it, it's going to make a material dent... it increases margins with less wastage and excess inventory and excess shipping and everything else."*

**Implication:** Startups targeting waste reduction in logistics, food service, and manufacturing are attacking massive, under-optimized markets with strong unit economics — AI makes the previously impractical suddenly tractable.

**IT is becoming the most strategically important team in the organization as companies begin building bespoke AI-powered software internally. This is a distinct function from traditional IT — it requires a software development orientation, not just infrastructure maintenance.** ([source](youtube:LionTree-podcast-Scott-Belsky-2026-predictions))

**Implication:** Organizations should start hiring for internal AI software development capability now — this team will be a source of competitive advantage, not just operational overhead.

**Enterprise software is undergoing a creativity-driven transition — for decades the dominant enterprise software trend was productivity applications (CRM, PowerPoint, Outlook). As creativity becomes a competitive necessity, the most vital enterprise software will be tools that enhance creative output.** ([source](nfx_founder_list:scott_belsky_creativity_productivity))

> *"For the last few decades one of the greatest technology trends in big enterprise has been the broad deployment of productivity applications from CRM software to PowerPoint and Outlook... as creativity becomes more important to both company and employee success the most vital software will be the applications that help you be more creative."*

**Implication:** Enterprise software vendors who reorient their roadmaps around enabling creative work — not just workflow efficiency — are positioned to win the next decade of enterprise spending.

**AI in film production has been a practical reality for years — features powered by AI have shipped in After Effects and Premiere Pro for nearly a decade. The current cultural panic about AI in Hollywood misframes a long-running incremental reality as a sudden existential threat.** ([source](EP 12 Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Practitioners who have been building with AI for years have a more calibrated and less reactive perspective on its implications — the loudest voices in the AI panic are often those who encountered it most recently.

**Contracts are the unrecognized sixth system of record — every revenue and expense item, every asset and liability on a company's balance sheet, originates from a contract. Yet most enterprises store this data as inert PDFs or physical files, making it unsearchable, unanalyzable, and commercially inert.** ([source](greycroft_mark_terbeek_outliers))

> *"Almost every revenue and expense item that you'll have that flows through your P&L or every asset and liability that you'll have as a company on your balance sheet is sitting in your contracts... that underlying data is what is the underlying asset value of all those contracts and therefore your revenues your costs your assets and your liabilities and it's not accessible it's not searchable it's not understandable it's not analyzable."*

**Implication:** The contract intelligence category is in early innings — any company that can unlock structured intelligence from contract data gains a genuine financial and operational advantage that compounds with every negotiation cycle.

**Revenue value chain — from customer acquisition through retention and success — is a core enterprise software investment theme because every business must master it, and the compounding advantage of doing it well is enormous.** ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

> *"Every company no matter what industry you're in has to be good at revenue acquisition and retention or you're going to be in trouble. The ones that really are great at it spend a lot on this to try to maintain an advantage and the ones that struggle end up having to spend a ton to try to catch up."*

**Implication:** Revenue operations software has a near-universal TAM because the problem it solves — acquiring and retaining customers efficiently — is industry-agnostic. Early leaders in this space benefit from compounding competitive advantages.

**Modern enterprises no longer want one monolithic software system.** Instead they want best-of-breed solutions that integrate with existing systems of record — making API-first architecture a non-negotiable requirement for new enterprise software. ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

> *"Enterprises in the future are now past wanting one monolithic piece of software like SAP to run everything. They're now matching best-of-breed providers that bring a certain level of functionality to the table but can still now integrate with the general ledger and the CRM system and the other core systems of record."*

**Implication:** API-first design is no longer a technical preference — it is the price of admission to the modern enterprise stack. Software that cannot integrate will be bypassed regardless of its standalone quality.

**Enterprise software buyers now demand fast time-to-value — measurable impact in 3-6 months — not 18-month implementation cycles. Startups that deliver rapid demonstrable value have a structural advantage over legacy enterprise vendors.** ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

> *"They want to be able to do it with a specific short-term time to value in mind where it's not this like 18 months and hope that it works kind of type implementation. It's got to be clear value in three months, six months at the most."*

**Implication:** Speed-to-value is now a core product design constraint for enterprise software, not just a sales consideration. Products that require long implementation cycles will lose to faster, more modular alternatives.

**The five traditional enterprise systems of record — CRM, HCM, ERP, service management, and general ledger — are largely claimed. The next wave of value creation comes from analytics, workflow, and intelligence layers built around these anchors.** ([source](#29 Greycroft: Investing Along the Frontier of Cloud Platforms and Enterprise Software | Mark Ter...))

**Implication:** Enterprise software founders should map their product against the existing systems-of-record landscape before positioning. The opportunity is increasingly in the intelligence and workflow layer, not in displacing entrenched systems.

**As vibe coding and AI-assisted development become mainstream, the IT department transforms from a cost center into the most strategically important team in any organization. Companies can now build tailor-made internal software without large engineering teams, meaning the people who understand both the business need and the technical tools become disproportionately valuable. The organizations that recognize this shift early and invest in their internal build capacity will develop moats that are invisible to competitors until it is too late.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Leaders should stop treating IT as overhead and start treating it as a product studio. The internal tools your team builds today are tomorrow's operational advantage.

**Belsky has observed that as AI makes code abundant, the security surface area of every organization expands dramatically. When anyone in a company can build and deploy software — through vibe coding or AI-assisted development — the perimeter that IT and security teams must defend grows faster than any centralized team can manage. This creates a new class of infrastructure risk that most organizations have not yet priced into their AI adoption strategies.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Security and governance frameworks need to scale with your AI build capacity, not lag behind it. Every new internal tool is a new attack surface; the policies for governing internally built AI software must be established before the tools proliferate.

**Belsky has described the coming wave of AI-native enterprise software as creating disposable or ephemeral software — applications built for a specific moment, workflow, or team that are meant to be replaced rather than maintained. This is a fundamental departure from the traditional enterprise software model of long procurement cycles and multi-year licensing, and it requires a completely different mental model for how organizations think about software investment and depreciation.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Build internal tools with the assumption they will be replaced in months, not years. Design for speed and specificity over permanence and generality — and stop applying legacy procurement logic to AI-native software decisions.

**Belsky has consistently argued that seat-based SaaS pricing is structurally broken in an agentic world, because AI agents do not occupy seats and cannot be priced the same way human users are. For enterprise buyers, this creates an immediate and significant leverage opportunity: as AI agents begin doing work previously done by licensed human users, the cost basis of enterprise software should fall, and buyers should be pushing vendors aggressively toward outcome-based pricing models. Companies that fail to renegotiate their vendor relationships in light of this shift are leaving money on the table.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Audit every per-seat SaaS contract your organization holds. Any workflow where AI is now doing work previously done by a licensed human is a negotiation opportunity — and a signal that you should be evaluating outcome-priced alternatives.

**Belsky has noted that the proliferation of AI tools inside organizations creates a new kind of alignment risk: when every team is building its own AI-assisted workflows and tools, the organizational knowledge that used to live in shared processes and systems becomes fragmented across dozens of locally optimized solutions. The companies that will extract the most value from internal AI building are those that pair the freedom to build locally with the discipline to integrate and share learnings across teams.** ([source](2026 Predictions with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Build an internal AI commons — a place where teams document what they have built, what worked, and what failed. Distributed building without knowledge sharing produces expensive duplication rather than compounding advantage.

**The commoditization of software development means that the bottleneck shifts from writing code to knowing what to build.** In the enterprise context, this elevates people with domain expertise and taste — those who understand workflows deeply enough to specify exactly what a custom tool should do — above people who can only execute on someone else's specification. The business analyst who can also direct an AI to build becomes one of the most valuable roles in any organization. ([source](How To Do More With Less People - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Invest in training domain experts to become AI-directed builders. The combination of workflow knowledge and AI fluency is rarer and more valuable than either skill alone.

**Belsky has argued that small teams augmented by AI can now accomplish what previously required hundreds of people — a dynamic he frames as the rise of the Small Team, Big Business model. In an enterprise context, this same principle applies to internal tooling: a two- or three-person team with access to AI coding tools can deploy workflow automation, custom dashboards, and agentic processes that previously required a dedicated software department. The cost of custom enterprise software collapses toward zero.** ([source](How To Do More With Less People - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Budget assumptions for internal software projects need to be completely rethought. What previously required a six-month, six-figure IT project may now take days and cost almost nothing.

**Agentic tools deployed inside organizations are beginning to replace entire categories of knowledge work rather than merely assist with them. Belsky has described this as a qualitative shift from AI as a copilot to AI as an autonomous operator — agents that don't just surface information but take action, complete workflows, and report back on outcomes. The organizational implication is that headcount planning must now account for work being done by systems rather than people.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** Start mapping your knowledge workflows to identify which ones are candidates for full agent replacement versus human-in-the-loop assistance. These are different design problems with different build priorities.

**Belsky has articulated a progression from AI chatbots to copilots to fully autonomous agents, and has positioned the copilot-to-agent transition as the most consequential leap for enterprise users. Copilots still require humans to direct every action and review every output; agents are trusted with sequences of decisions and can operate across multiple systems without step-by-step oversight. Organizations that have not built the internal trust frameworks — both technical and cultural — to deploy agents at this level will fall behind those that have.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** The infrastructure work needed to safely deploy autonomous agents — data access controls, audit trails, escalation protocols — should be started now, before you think you need it.

**Belsky has emphasized that the interface layer, not the underlying model, is where value is captured in AI-powered products — and this principle applies with particular force to internal enterprise tools. An AI model accessed through a generic chat interface will be used generically; the same model embedded in a purpose-built workflow tool that meets employees where they already work will drive transformative adoption. The organization that invests in building the right interface around AI captures far more value than one that simply provides API access.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Don't just give employees access to AI — build the interface that makes AI feel like a natural extension of the specific work they already do. Interface design is not a cosmetic decision; it is a strategic one.

**Revenue per employee has emerged in Belsky's framework as the defining metric of the AI-augmented enterprise.** He has argued that organizations deploying AI effectively should expect dramatic increases in output per person rather than modest efficiency gains — and that companies still measuring AI success by time saved on individual tasks are measuring the wrong thing entirely. The strategic question is not whether AI is saving hours but whether AI is changing the denominator in the revenue-per-employee equation. ([source](How To Do More With Less People - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Reframe your AI ROI metrics. If your AI investments are not meaningfully moving revenue per employee, you are probably deploying AI tactically rather than structurally.

**Belsky has noted that the creative tools revolution pioneered in consumer-facing software — real-time collaboration, AI generation, direct manipulation interfaces — is now migrating into enterprise workflow software. Adobe's own trajectory under his product leadership reflected this: the company consistently pushed the frontier of AI-assisted creative tools before many enterprise software vendors had begun serious AI integration. The consumer-to-enterprise migration of AI tooling means that the most advanced internal tools often look more like creative software than like traditional business applications.** ([source](Adobe's Scott Belsky: Art & design in the age of AI | Rapid Response))

**Implication:** Look to consumer-grade AI tools for product inspiration when designing internal enterprise systems. The interaction paradigms being established in creative software will define what enterprise users expect next.

**In Belsky's view, the first mile principle applies as much to internal enterprise tools as it does to consumer products.** Enterprise software has historically gotten away with terrible onboarding because employees had no choice but to use mandated systems. But as employees gain access to AI tools and vibe-coded alternatives, the bar for internal tool adoption rises sharply — workers will route around clunky systems, and shadow IT built with AI will proliferate. Internal product teams now have to compete for employee attention, not just mandate it. ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Apply consumer-grade product thinking to internal tools. If your enterprise software cannot hook employees in the first 30 seconds, they will build or find something better — with or without IT's blessing.

**Belsky has framed taste as the scarce resource in an AI-abundant world, and this applies directly to enterprise AI deployment: the question is no longer whether your organization can build an AI tool but whether you have people with sufficient taste to know which tools are worth building and which workflows are worth automating. Organizations that can consistently make the right build/buy/automate decisions will compound their AI advantage, while those that automate indiscriminately will create technical debt and organizational confusion at AI speed.** ([source](Interview with Scott Belsky: GenAI - Creativity as the Now Productivity))

**Implication:** Identify the people in your organization with the strongest combination of workflow knowledge and discernment — they are your de facto AI product managers, whether or not they have that title.

**Belsky has observed that AI is transforming what it means to be a knowledge worker by collapsing the gap between thinking and making. In an enterprise context, this means that the employee who used to spend most of their time executing on a plan — writing reports, building presentations, coding analyses — can now spend most of their time on the judgment and direction work that was previously reserved for senior roles. The org chart implications of this shift are profound and most organizations have not begun to reckon with them.** ([source](Scott Belsky on AI and the Return of a Hyper-Personalized World))

**Implication:** Redesign roles around judgment and direction, not execution. The knowledge worker job description of 2025 should look nothing like it did in 2020 — and if yours still does, you are underusing your people and your AI.

**Belsky has argued that resourcefulness — the capacity to do more with less — is a muscle built through constraint, and that organizations which develop genuine resourcefulness through early AI adoption will outperform those that simply throw AI tools at existing headcount. The risk in enterprise AI adoption is not moving too fast but moving too comfortably: using AI to make existing expensive processes slightly cheaper rather than using AI to fundamentally reimagine what those processes should be.** ([source](How To Do More With Less People - Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** Use AI as an opportunity to question whether workflows should exist at all, not just to make existing workflows faster. The most valuable AI wins in enterprise come from elimination, not optimization.

**Belsky has described the prompt interface as a transitional — not terminal — form for AI interaction, and has anticipated a shift toward direct manipulation controls that feel like natural extensions of existing workflows. In enterprise software, this suggests that the current generation of AI tools that require employees to learn prompt engineering are not the final form: the winning internal tools will be the ones that embed AI assistance directly into the workflows employees already use, requiring no new interaction paradigm at all.** ([source](Adobe's Scott Belsky: Art & design in the age of AI | Rapid Response))

**Implication:** Prioritize AI features that feel invisible over ones that require employees to learn a new way of working. The most adopted enterprise AI will be the AI that employees barely notice they are using.

**Belsky has pointed to the convergence of AI capabilities with cloud infrastructure as the enabling condition for a new wave of enterprise software creation. The same forces that allowed Behance to be built by a tiny team — cheap infrastructure, open APIs, accessible design tools — are now available for AI-native internal tool building at a scale that makes custom enterprise software economically viable for any organization, not just large ones with dedicated engineering departments.** ([source](Building Adobe & the Future of Design with Scott Belsky))

**Implication:** The playbook for internal tool building that previously only applied to tech companies now applies to every organization. The question is not whether you can afford to build custom tools — it is whether you can afford not to.

**Belsky has consistently warned against the organizational tendency to over-explain rather than over-do — what he frames as the Do-Show-Explain hierarchy. This principle has sharp implications for enterprise AI deployment: most internal AI rollouts are dominated by training sessions, documentation, and change management communications rather than by simply doing the work for employees and letting them experience the benefit directly. The organizations that let AI demonstrate its value through action rather than explanation will achieve faster and more durable adoption.** ([source](Lessons on product sense, AI, the first mile experience, and the messy middle | Scott Belsky (Adobe)))

**Implication:** Redesign your internal AI rollout strategy around demonstration rather than education. Build one workflow that AI completes entirely and show employees the output before you explain how it works.

**Belsky has highlighted that as AI agents begin operating with more autonomy inside organizations, the human roles that remain most valuable are those involving oversight, curation, and taste — deciding which agent outputs are good enough to act on, which workflows need human review, and which automated decisions require escalation. This is a fundamentally different kind of managerial work than most organizations have trained their people for, and building this capacity is itself a strategic investment.** ([source](Scott Belsky & Steve Jang: From AI Chatbots to Copilots))

**Implication:** Start developing 'agent manager' capabilities in your most trusted operators now. The skill of evaluating, directing, and correcting AI agent behavior is not intuitive — it requires deliberate training and experience.

**Belsky has framed Adobe's own transformation as a case study in how a legacy creative software company can reinvent itself around AI-native tools without losing the trust of its core user base. The key was integrating AI into existing workflows in ways that felt like natural extensions of the tools creatives already used — Photoshop, Premiere, Firefly — rather than launching standalone AI products that required behavior change. This inside-out approach to AI integration applies directly to enterprise software strategy: augment the tools employees already trust rather than asking them to adopt new ones.** ([source](Adobe's Scott Belsky: Art & design in the age of AI | Rapid Response))

**Implication:** Map your AI deployment strategy to tools and workflows that already have high internal adoption. The path of least resistance for enterprise AI is through beloved existing tools, not through mandated new ones.

**Belsky has argued that the most dangerous organizational response to AI abundance is adding process rather than adding judgment. As AI tools proliferate inside enterprises, the instinct is to layer governance, approval workflows, and compliance procedures on top of them. But in his framework, process is a symptom of misalignment, not a solution to it — and organizations that respond to the complexity of AI deployment with more process will slow down precisely when they need to move fast.** ([source](The Messy Middle))

**Implication:** Build AI governance around clear principles and decision rights, not around approval chains and checklists. Teams that are genuinely aligned on what they are trying to accomplish need very little process to deploy AI responsibly.

**Belsky has observed that the most dangerous phase of any new technology deployment inside a large organization is the messy middle — the period after initial excitement when the hard integration work begins and early results are mixed. Enterprise AI adoption is currently in this phase for most organizations: the pilots went well, the board is excited, but the actual embedding of AI into core workflows has produced more friction than expected. The organizations that endure through this phase rather than retreating to safety will emerge with durable advantages.** ([source](The Messy Middle))

**Implication:** Treat uneven AI adoption results as a signal to persist and problem-solve, not to pull back. The messy middle of AI transformation is where most competitors will quit — which is precisely why staying in it is strategically valuable.
